# Task: Generate API Reference Documentation for Kafka KafkaConsumer

## Objective

Generate comprehensive API reference documentation for the Apache Kafka `KafkaConsumer` Java API. The documentation should cover the complete API surface with emphasis on behavioral semantics, offset management strategies, rebalancing mechanics, and error handling patterns.

## Scope

Your documentation should cover the **KafkaConsumer API** from the `org.apache.kafka.clients.consumer` package, including:

1. **Core Consumer Lifecycle**
   - Constructor variants and configuration
   - Subscription methods (dynamic and manual assignment)
   - Polling and data fetching semantics
   - Resource cleanup and closing

2. **Offset Management**
   - Synchronous and asynchronous commit strategies
   - Offset queries and position control
   - Seek operations and offset discovery
   - Committed offset semantics

3. **Consumer Group Mechanics**
   - ConsumerRebalanceListener interface and callbacks
   - Rebalance triggers and timing
   - Group membership and heartbeat behavior
   - Partition assignment vs subscription models

4. **Flow Control and Position Management**
   - Pause and resume functionality
   - Position queries and manipulation
   - Offset-to-timestamp lookups
   - Beginning and end offset discovery

5. **Error Handling**
   - Exception types and recovery strategies
   - CommitFailedException and group fencing
   - WakeupException and thread interruption
   - Timeout and authentication errors

## Requirements

### API Methods Documentation (40%)

Document all public methods of the `KafkaConsumer` class with:
- Method signatures including all overloads
- Parameter semantics and validation rules
- Return types and their meanings
- Exception types thrown and conditions

### Behavioral Notes (30%)

Explain critical behavioral semantics:
- **poll() blocking behavior**: when it returns immediately vs when it blocks, timeout handling, rebalance callback execution during poll
- **Offset commit semantics**: difference between sync/async commits, retry behavior, commit failure handling
- **Rebalance coordination**: when rebalances occur (only during poll), callback ordering (revoked then assigned), partition ownership guarantees
- **Thread safety**: which methods are thread-safe, wakeup() special case, event loop model
- **Group membership**: max.poll.interval.ms enforcement, proactive leave behavior, session timeout vs poll timeout
- **Manual vs dynamic assignment**: mutually exclusive nature, use cases for each model
- **Position vs committed offset**: the off-by-one relationship ("committed should be next offset to read")
- **Transactional semantics**: read_committed isolation level, LSO boundary, filtered messages

### Usage Examples (20%)

Provide concrete code examples demonstrating:
- **Basic subscription and polling loop**: subscribe to topics, poll for records, process messages
- **Manual offset commit**: disable auto-commit, explicit commitSync/commitAsync after processing
- **Rebalance listener**: implement ConsumerRebalanceListener, commit offsets on revoke, initialize positions on assign
- **Seek operations**: seekToBeginning, seekToEnd, seek to specific offset, timestamp-based seeking
- **Multi-threaded processing pattern**: single consumer thread with pause/resume coordination and worker pool

### Documentation Structure (10%)

Organize documentation with clear sections:
- Overview and threading model
- Core types (KafkaConsumer, ConsumerRebalanceListener, ConsumerRecords, etc.)
- Subscription and assignment methods
- Polling and data fetching
- Offset management methods
- Flow control and position queries
- Metadata and monitoring
- Lifecycle and resource management
- Exception handling guide
- Configuration-driven behaviors
- Common patterns and best practices

## Deliverable

Write your documentation to `/workspace/documentation.md` in Markdown format.

## Notes

- Focus on **behavioral semantics** that aren't obvious from method signatures alone
- Include edge cases and gotchas (e.g., poll may block longer than timeout during rebalance callbacks)
- Explain the relationship between different offset concepts (position, committed, beginning, end, LSO)
- Cover both group-managed and standalone consumer patterns
- Document configuration properties that significantly affect API behavior
- Use the codebase to find real usage patterns in tests and internal components

## Evaluation

Your documentation will be evaluated on:
1. **Completeness**: All key API methods and types documented
2. **Accuracy**: Behavioral descriptions match actual implementation
3. **Clarity**: Complex semantics explained clearly with examples
4. **Practical value**: Real-world usage patterns and error handling strategies included

# Apache Kafka KafkaConsumer API Reference

## Table of Contents
1. [Overview](#overview)
2. [Core Types](#core-types)
3. [Consumer Lifecycle](#consumer-lifecycle)
4. [Subscription and Assignment](#subscription-and-assignment)
5. [Polling and Data Fetching](#polling-and-data-fetching)
6. [Offset Management](#offset-management)
7. [Flow Control and Position Management](#flow-control-and-position-management)
8. [Metadata and Monitoring](#metadata-and-monitoring)
9. [Rebalancing Mechanics](#rebalancing-mechanics)
10. [Error Handling](#error-handling)
11. [Multi-threaded Processing](#multi-threaded-processing)
12. [Transactional Message Reading](#transactional-message-reading)
13. [Usage Patterns](#usage-patterns)
14. [Configuration Guide](#configuration-guide)

---

## Overview

The `KafkaConsumer` is a Java client for consuming records from a Kafka cluster. It provides a flexible consumption model supporting:

- **Group-managed consumption**: Automatic partition assignment across consumer group members
- **Manual assignment**: Direct partition assignment with no group coordination
- **Dynamic subscriptions**: Pattern-based topic subscriptions
- **Flexible offset management**: Automatic or manual commit strategies
- **Metadata queries**: Partition discovery and offset inspection
- **Flow control**: Pause/resume consumption on specific partitions

### Key Principles

1. **Not Thread-Safe**: The consumer is designed for single-threaded use. All methods except `wakeup()` require exclusive access.
2. **Event-Driven Model**: Rebalancing and callbacks occur during `poll()` calls only.
3. **Offset-Based Positioning**: Consumption is controlled via offsets within partitions.
4. **Blocking Semantics**: Various operations may block (networking, broke
... (truncated)

✅ **Behavioral Semantics** (30% - Critical Implementation Details)
- **Poll blocking behavior**: When it returns immediately vs blocks beyond timeout, rebalance callback execution
- **Offset commit semantics**: Sync/async differences, retry behavior, committed position format
- **Rebalance coordination**: When occurs (poll only), callback ordering (revoked→assigned), partition ownership
- **Thread safety**: Which methods thread-safe, `wakeup()` special case, event loop model
- **Group membership**: `max.poll.interval.ms` enforcement, proactive leave, session timeout vs poll timeout
- **Manual vs dynamic assignment**: Mutually exclusive nature, use cases
- **Position vs committed offset**: Critical relationship ("committed should be next offset")
- **Transactional semantics**: `read_committed` isolation, LSO boundary, filtered messages

✅ **Usage Examples** (20% - Real-world Patterns)
- Basic subscription with auto-commit
- Manual offset commit with batch processing
- Rebalance listener for external offset storage
- Multi-threaded patterns (single consumer thread + worker pool)
- Pause/resum

governing permissions and\n15:  * limitations under the License.\n16:  */\n17: package org.apache.kafka.clients.consumer;\n18: \n19: import org.apache.kafka.clients.KafkaClient;\n20: import org.apache.kafka.clients.consumer.internals.ConsumerDelegate;\n21: import org.apache.kafka.clients.consumer.internals.ConsumerDelegateCreator;\n22: import org.apache.kafka.clients.consumer.internals.ConsumerMetadata;\n23: import org.apache.kafka.clients.consumer.internals.SubscriptionState;\n24: import org.apache.kafka.clients.consumer.internals.metrics.KafkaConsumerMetrics;\n25: import org.apache.kafka.common.KafkaException;\n26: import org.apache.kafka.common.Metric;\n27: import org.apache.kafka.common.MetricName;\n28: import org.apache.kafka.common.PartitionInfo;\n29: import org.apache.kafka.common.TopicPartition;\n30: import org.apache.kafka.common.Uuid;\n31: import org.apache.kafka.common.errors.InterruptException;\n32: import org.apache.kafka.common.errors.InvalidRegularExpression;\n33: import org.apache.kafka.common.errors.WakeupException;\n34: import org.apache.kafka.common.metrics.KafkaMetric;\n35: import org.apache.kafka.common.metrics.Metrics;\n36: import org.apache.kafka.common.serialization.Deserializer;\n37: import org.apache.kafka.common.utils.LogContext;\n38: import org.apache.kafka.common.utils.Time;\n39: import org.apache.kafka.common.utils.Timer;\n40: \n41: import java.time.Duration;\n42: import java.util.Collection;\n43: import java.util.ConcurrentModificationException;\n44: import java.util.List;\n45: import java.util.Map;\n46: import java.util.OptionalLong;\n47: import java.util.Properties;\n48: import java.util.Set;\n49: import java.util.regex.Pattern;\n50: \n51: import static org.apache.kafka.common.utils.Utils.propsToMap;\n52: \n53: /**\n54:  * A client that consumes records from a Kafka cluster.\n55:  * <p>\n56:  * This client transparently handles the failure of Kafka brokers, and transparently adapts as topic partitions\n57:  * it fetches migrate within the cluster. This client also interacts with the broker to allow groups of\n58:  * consumers to load balance consumption using <a href=\"#consumergroups\">consumer groups</a>.\n59:  * <p>\n60:  * The consumer maintains TCP connections to the necessary brokers to fetch data.\n61:  * Failure to close the consumer after use will leak these connections.\n62:  * The consumer is not thread-safe. See <a href=\"#multithreaded\">Multi-threaded Processing</a> for more details.\n63:  *\n64:  * <h3>Cross-Version Compatibility</h3>\n65:  * This client can communicate with brokers that are version 0.10.0 or newer. Older or newer brokers may not support\n66:  * certain features. For example, 0.10.0 brokers do not support offsetsForTimes, because this feature was added\n67:  * in version 0.10.1. You will receive an {@link org.apache.kafka.common.errors.UnsupportedVersionException}\n68:  * when invoking an API that is not available on the running broker version.\n69:  * <p>\n70:  *\n71:  * <h3>Offsets and Consumer Position</h3>\n72:  * Kafka maintains a numerical offset for each record in a partition. This offset acts as a unique identifier of\n73:  * a record within that partition, and also denotes the position of the consumer in the partition. For example, a consumer\n74:  * which is at position 5 has consumed records with offsets 0 through 4 and will next receive the record with offset 5.\n75:  * Note that offsets are not guaranteed to be consecutive (such as compacted topic or when records have been produced\n76:  * using transactions). For example, if the consumer did read a record with offset 4, but 5 is not an offset\n77:  * with a record, its position might advance to 6 (or higher) directly. Similarly, if the consumer's position is 5,\n78:  * but there is no record with offset 5, the consumer will return the record with the next higher offset.\n79:  * There are actually two notions of position relevant to the user of the consumer:\n80:  * <p>\n81:  * The {@link #position(TopicPartition) position} of the consumer gives the offset of the next record that will be given\n82:  * out. It will be one larger than the highest offset the consumer has seen in that partition. It automatically advances\n83:  * every time the consumer receives messages in a call to {@link #poll(Duration)}.\n84:  * <p>\n85:  * The {@link #commitSync() committed position} is the last offset that has been stored securely. Should the\n86:  * process fail and restart, this is the offset that the consumer will recover to. The consumer can either automatically commit\n87:  * offsets periodically; or it can choose to control this committed position manually by calling one of the commit APIs\n88:  * (e.g. {@link #commitSync() commitSync} and {@link #commitAsync(OffsetCommitCallback) commitAsync}).\n89:  * <p>\n90:  * This distinction gives the consumer control over when a record is considered consumed. It is discussed in further\n91:  * detail below.\n92:  *\n93:  * <h3><a name=\"consumergroups\">Consumer Groups and Topic Subscriptions</a></h3>\n94:  *\n95:  * Kafka uses the concept of <i>consumer groups</i> to allow a pool of processes to divide the work of consuming and\n96:  * processing records. These processes can either be running on the same machine or they can be\n97:  * distributed over many machines to provide scalability and fault tolerance for processing. All consumer instances\n98:  * sharing the same {@code group.id} will be part of the same consumer group.\n99:  * <p>\n100:  * Each consumer in a group can dynamically set the list of topics it wants to subscribe to through one of the\n101:  * {@link #subscribe(Collection, ConsumerRebalanceListener) subscribe} APIs. Kafka will deliver each message in the\n102:  * subscribed topics to one process in each consumer group. This is achieved by balancing the partitions between all\n103:  * members in the consumer group so that each partition is assigned to exactly one consumer in the group. So if there\n104:  * is a topic with four partitions, and a consumer group with two processes, each process would consume from two partitions.\n105:  * <p>\n106:  * Membership in a consumer group is maintained dynamically: if a process fails, the partitions assigned to it will\n107:  * be reassigned to other consumers in the same group. Similarly, if a new consumer joins the group, partitions will be moved\n108:  * from existing consumers to the new one. This is known as <i>rebalancing</i> the group and is discussed in more\n109:  * detail <a href=\"#failuredetection\">below</a>. Group rebalancing is also used when new partitions are added\n110:  * to one of the subscribed topics or when a new topic matching a {@link #subscribe(Pattern, ConsumerRebalanceListener) subscribed regex}\n111:  * is created. The group will automatically detect the new partitions through periodic metadata refreshes and\n112:  * assign them to members of the group.\n113:  * <p>\n114:  * Conceptually you can think of a consumer group as being a single logical subscriber that happens to be made up of\n115:  * multiple processes. As a multi-subscriber system, Kafka naturally supports having any number of consumer groups for a\n116:  * given topic without duplicating data (additional consumers are actually quite cheap).\n117:  * <p>\n118:  * This is a slight generalization of the functionality that is common in messaging systems. To get semantics similar to\n119:  * a queue in a traditional messaging system all processes would be part of a single consumer group and hence record\n120:  * delivery would be balanced over the group like with a queue. Unlike a traditional messaging system, though, you can\n121:  * have multiple such groups. To get semantics similar to pub-sub in a traditional messaging system each process would\n122:  * have its own consumer group, so each process would subscribe to all the records published to the topic.\n123:  * <p>\n124:  * In addition, when group reassignment happens automatically, consumers can be notified through a {@link ConsumerRebalanceListener},\n125:  * which allows them to finish necessary application-level logic such as state cleanup, manual offset\n126:  * commits, etc. See <a href=\"#rebalancecallback\">Storing Offsets Outside Kafka</a> for more details.\n127:  * <p>\n128:  * It is also possible for the consumer to <a href=\"#manualassignment\">manually assign</a> specific partitions\n129:  * (similar to the older \"simple\" consumer) using {@link #assign(Collection)}. In this case, dynamic partition\n130:  * assignment and consumer group coordination will be disabled.\n131:  *\n132:  * <h3><a name=\"failuredetection\">Detecting Consumer Failures</a></h3>\n133:  *\n134:  * After subscribing to a set of topics, the consumer will automatically join the group when {@link #poll(Duration)} is\n135:  * invoked. The poll API is designed to ensure consumer liveness. As long as you continue to call poll, the consumer\n136:  * will stay in the group and continue to receive messages from the partitions it was assigned. Underneath the covers,\n137:  * the consumer sends periodic heartbeats to the server. If the consumer crashes or is unable to send heartbeats for\n138:  * a duration of {@code session.timeout.ms}, then the consumer will be considered dead and its partitions will\n139:  * be reassigned.\n140:  * <p>\n141:  * It is also possible that the consumer could encounter a \"livelock\" situation where it is continuing\n142:  * to send heartbeats, but no progress is being made. To prevent the consumer from holding onto its partitions\n143:  * indefinitely in this case, we provide a liveness detection mechanism using the {@code max.poll.interval.ms}\n144:  * setting. Basically if you don't call poll at least as frequently as the configured max interval,\n145:  * then the client will proactively leave the group so that another consumer can take over its partitions. When this happens,\n146:  * you may see an offset commit failure (as indicated by a {@link CommitFailedException} thrown from a call to {@link #commitSync()}).\n147:  * This is a safety mechanism which guarantees that only active members of the group are able to commit offsets.\n148:  * So to stay in the group, you must continue to call poll.\n149:  * <p>\n150:  * The consumer provides two configuration settings to control the behavior of the poll loop:\n151:  * <ol>\n152:  *     <li><code>max.poll.interval.ms</code>: By increasing the interval between expected polls, you can give\n153:  *     the consumer more time to handle a batch of records returned from {@link #poll(Duration)}. The drawback\n154:  *     is that increasing this value may delay a group rebalance since the consumer will only join the rebalance\n155:  *     inside the call to poll. You can use this setting to bound the time to finish a rebalance, but\n156:  *     you risk slower progress if the consumer cannot actually call {@link #poll(Duration) poll} often enough.</li>\n157:  *     <li><code>max.poll.records</code>: Use this setting to limit the total records returned from a single\n158:  *     call to poll. This can make it easier to predict the maximum that must be handled within each poll\n159:  *     interval. By tuning this value, you may be able to reduce the poll interval, which will reduce the\n160:  *     impact of group rebalancing.</li>\n161:  * </ol>\n162:  * <p>\n163:  * For use cases where message processing time varies unpredictably, neither of these options may be sufficient.\n164:  * The recommended way to handle these cases is to move message processing to another thread, which allows\n165:  * the consumer to continue calling {@link #poll(Duration) poll} while the processor is still working.\n166:  * Some care must be taken to ensure that committed offsets do not get ahead of the actual position.\n167:  * Typically, you must disable automatic commits and manually commit processed offsets for records only after the\n168:  * thread has finished handling them (depending on the delivery semantics you need).\n169:  * Note also that you will need to {@link #pause(Collection) pause} the partition so that no new records are received\n170:  * from poll until after thread has finished handling those previously returned.\n171:  *\n172:  * <h3>Usage Examples</h3>\n173:  * The consumer APIs offer flexibility to cover a variety of consumption use cases. Here are some examples to\n174:  * demonstrate how to use them.\n175:  *\n176:  * <h4>Automatic Offset Committing</h4>\n177:  * This example demonstrates a simple usage of Kafka's consumer api that relies on automatic offset committing.\n178:  * <p>\n179:  * <pre>\n180:  * {@code\n181:  *     Properties props = new Properties();\n182:  *     props.setProperty(\"bootstrap.servers\", \"localhost:9092\");\n183:  *     props.setProperty(\"group.id\", \"test\");\n184:  *     props.setProperty(\"enable.auto.commit\", \"true\");\n185:  *     props.setProperty(\"auto.commit.interval.ms\", \"1000\");\n186:  *     props.setProperty(\"key.deserializer\", \"org.apache.kafka.common.serialization.StringDeserializer\");\n187:  *     props.setProperty(\"value.deserializer\", \"org.apache.kafka.common.serialization.StringDeserializer\");\n188:  *     KafkaConsumer<String, String> consumer = new KafkaConsumer<>(props);\n189:  *     consumer.subscribe(Arrays.asList(\"foo\", \"bar\"));\n190:  *     while (true) {\n191:  *         ConsumerRecords<String, String> records = consumer.poll(Duration.ofMillis(100));\n192:  *         for (ConsumerRecord<String, String> record : records)\n193:  *             System.out.printf(\"offset = %d, key = %s, value = %s%n\", record.offset(), record.key(), record.value());\n194:  *     }\n195:  * }</pre>\n196:  *\n197:  * The connection to the cluster is bootstrapped by specifying a list of one or more brokers to contact using the\n198:  * configuration {@code bootstrap.servers}. This list is just used to discover the rest of the brokers in the\n199:  * cluster and need not be an exhaustive list of servers in the cluster (though you may want to specify more than one in\n200:  * case there are servers down when the client is connecting).\n201:  * <p>\n202:  * Setting {@code enable.auto.commit} means that offsets are committed automatically with a frequency controlled by\n203:  * the config {@code auto.commit.interval.ms}.\n204:  * <p>\n205:  * In this example the consumer is subscribing to the topics <i>foo</i> and <i>bar</i> as part of a group of consumers\n206:  * called <i>test</i> as configured with {@code group.id}.\n207:  * <p>\n208:  * The deserializer settings specify how to turn bytes into objects. For example, by specifying string deserializers, we\n209:  * are saying that our record's key and value will just be simple strings.\n210:  *\n211:  * <h4>Manual Offset Control</h4>\n212:  *\n213:  * Instead of relying on the consumer to periodically commit consumed offsets, users can also control when records\n214:  * should be considered as consumed and hence commit their offsets. This is useful when the consumption of the messages\n215:  * is coupled with some processing logic and hence a message should not be considered as consumed until it is completed processing.\n216: \n217:  * <p>\n218:  * <pre>\n219:  * {@code\n220:  *     Properties props = new Properties();\n221:  *     props.setProperty(\"bootstrap.servers\", \"localhost:9092\");\n222:  *     props.setProperty(\"group.id\", \"test\");\n223:  *     props.setProperty(\"enable.auto.commit\", \"false\");\n224:  *     props.setProperty(\"key.deserializer\", \"org.apache.kafka.common.serialization.StringDeserializer\");\n225:  *     props.setProperty(\"value.deserializer\", \"org.apache.kafka.common.serialization.StringDeserializer\");\n226:  *     KafkaConsumer<String, String> consumer = new KafkaConsumer<>(props);\n227:  *     consumer.subscribe(Arrays.asList(\"foo\", \"bar\"));\n228:  *     final int minBatchSize = 200;\n229:  *     List<ConsumerRecord<String, String>> buffer = new ArrayList<>();\n230:  *     while (true) {\n231:  *         ConsumerRecords<String, String> records = consumer.poll(Duration.ofMillis(100));\n232:  *         for (ConsumerRecord<String, String> record : records) {\n233:  *             buffer.add(record);\n234:  *         }\n235:  *         if (buffer.size() >= minBatchSize) {\n236:  *             insertIntoDb(buffer);\n237:  *             consumer.commitSync();\n238:  *             buffer.clear();\n239:  *         }\n240:  *     }\n241:  * }</pre>\n242:  *\n243:  * In this example we will consume a batch of records and batch them up in memory. When we have enough records\n244:  * batched, we will insert them into a database. If we allowed offsets to auto commit as in the previous example, records\n245:  * would be considered consumed after they were returned to the user in {@link #poll(Duration) poll}. It would then be\n246:  * possible\n247:  * for our process to fail after batching the records, but before they had been inserted into the database.\n248:  * <p>\n249:  * To avoid this, we will manually commit the offsets only after the corresponding records have been inserted into the\n250:  * database. This gives us exact control of when a record is considered consumed. This raises the opposite possibility:\n251:  * the process could fail in the interval after the insert into the database but before the commit (even though this\n252:  * would likely just be a few milliseconds, it is a possibility). In this case the process that took over consumption\n253:  * would consume from last committed offset and would repeat the insert of the last batch of data. Used in this way\n254:  * Kafka provides what is often called \"at-least-once\" delivery guarantees, as each record will likely be delivered one\n255:  * time but in failure cases could be duplicated.\n256:  * <p>\n257:  * <b>Note: Using automatic offset commits can also give you \"at-least-once\" delivery, but the requirement is that\n258:  * you must consume all data returned from each call to {@link #poll(Duration)} before any subsequent calls, or before\n259:  * {@link #close() closing} the consumer. If you fail to do either of these, it is possible for the committed offset\n260:  * to get ahead of the consumed position, which results in missing records. The advantage of using manual offset\n261:  * control is that you have direct control over when a record is considered \"consumed.\"</b>\n262:  * <p>\n263:  * The above example uses {@link #commitSync() commitSync} to mark all received records as committed. In some cases\n264:  * you may wish to have even finer control over which records have been committed by specifying an offset explicitly.\n265:  * In the example below we commit offset after we finish handling the records in each partition.\n266:  * <p>\n267:  * <pre>\n268:  * {@code\n269:  *     try {\n270:  *         while(running) {\n271:  *             ConsumerRecords<String, String> records = consumer.poll(Duration.ofMillis(Long.MAX_VALUE));\n272:  *             for (TopicPartition partition : records.partitions()) {\n273:  *                 List<ConsumerRecord<String, String>> partitionRecords = records.records(partition);\n274:  *                 for (ConsumerRecord<String, String> record : partitionRecords) {\n275:  *                     System.out.println(record.offset() + \": \" + record.value());\n276:  *                 }\n277:  *                 consumer.commitSync(Collections.singletonMap(partition, records.nextOffsets().get(partition)));\n278:  *             }\n279:  *         }\n280:  *     } finally {\n281:  *       consumer.close();\n282:  *     }\n283:  * }</pre>\n284:  *\n285:  * <b>Note: The committed offset should always be the offset of the next message that your application will read.</b>\n286:  * Thus, when calling {@link #commitSync(Map) commitSync(offsets)} you should use {@code nextRecordToBeProcessed.offset()}\n287:  * or if {@link ConsumerRecords} is exhausted already {@link ConsumerRecords#nextOffsets()} instead.\n288:  * You should also add the leader epoch as commit metadata, which can be obtained from\n289:  * {@link ConsumerRecord#leaderEpoch()} or {@link ConsumerRecords#nextOffsets()}.\n290:  *\n291:  * <h4><a name=\"manualassignment\">Manual Partition Assignment</a></h4>\n292:  *\n293:  * In the previous examples, we subscribed to the topics we were interested in and let Kafka dynamically assign a\n294:  * fair share of the partitions for those topics based on the active consumers in the group. However, in\n295:  * some cases you may need finer control over the specific partitions that are assigned. For example:\n296:  * <p>\n297:  * <ul>\n298:  * <li>If the process is maintaining some kind of local state associated with that partition (like a\n299:  * local on-disk key-value store), then it should only get records for the partition it is maintaining on disk.\n300:  * <li>If the process itself is highly available and will be restarted if it fails (perhaps using a\n301:  * cluster management framework like YARN, Mesos, or AWS facilities, or as part of a stream processing framework). In\n302:  * this case there is no need for Kafka to detect the failure and reassign the partition since the consuming process\n303:  * will be restarted on another machine.\n304:  * </ul>\n305:  * <p>\n306:  * To use this mode, instead of subscribing to the topic using {@link #subscribe(Collection) subscribe}, you just call\n307:  * {@link #assign(Collection)} with the full list of partitions that you want to consume.\n308:  *\n309:  * <pre>\n310:  * {@code\n311:  *     String topic = \"foo\";\n312:  *     TopicPartition partition0 = new TopicPartition(topic, 0);\n313:  *     TopicPartition partition1 = new TopicPartition(topic, 1);\n314:  *     consumer.assign(Arrays.asList(partition0, partition1));\n315:  * }</pre>\n316:  *\n317:  * Once assigned, you can call {@link #poll(Duration) poll} in a loop, just as in the preceding examples to consume\n318:  * records. The group that the consumer specifies is still used for committing offsets, but now the set of partitions\n319:  * will only change with another call to {@link #assign(Collection) assign}. Manual partition assignment does\n320:  * not use group coordination, so consumer failures will not cause assigned partitions to be rebalanced. Each consumer\n321:  * acts independently even if it shares a groupId with another consumer. To avoid offset commit conflicts, you should\n322:  * usually ensure that the groupId is unique for each consumer instance.\n323:  * <p>\n324:  * Note that it isn't possible to mix manual partition assignment (i.e. using {@link #assign(Collection) assign})\n325:  * with dynamic partition assignment through topic subscription (i.e. using {@link #subscribe(Collection) subscribe}).\n326:  *\n327:  * <h4><a name=\"rebalancecallback\">Storing Offsets Outside Kafka</h4>\n328:  *\n329:  * The consumer application need not use Kafka's built-in offset storage, it can store offsets in a store of its own\n330:  * choosing. The primary use case for this is allowing the application to store both the offset and the results of the\n331:  * consumption in the same system in a way that both the results and offsets are stored atomically. This is not always\n332:  * possible, but when it is it will make the consumption fully atomic and give \"exactly once\" semantics that are\n333:  * stronger than the default \"at-least once\" semantics you get with Kafka's offset commit functionality.\n334:  * <p>\n335:  * Here are a couple of examples of this type of usage:\n336:  * <ul>\n337:  * <li>If the results of the consumption are being stored in a relational database, storing the offset in the database\n338:  * as well can allow committing both the results and offset in a single transaction. Thus either the transaction will\n339:  * succeed and the offset will be updated based on what was consumed or the result will not be stored and the offset\n340:  * won't be updated.\n341:  * <li>If the results are being stored in a local store it may be possible to store the offset there as well. For\n342:  * example a search index could be built by subscribing to a particular partition and storing both the offset and the\n343:  * indexed data together. If this is done in a way that is atomic, it is often possible to have it be the case that even\n344:  * if a crash occurs that causes unsync'd data to be lost, whatever is left has the corresponding offset stored as well.\n345:  * This means that in this case the indexing process that comes back having lost recent updates just resumes indexing\n346:  * from what it has ensuring that no updates are lost.\n347:  * </ul>\n348:  * <p>\n349:  * Each record comes with its own offset, so to manage your own offset you just need to do the following:\n350:  *\n351:  * <ul>\n352:  * <li>Configure <code>enable.auto.commit=false</code>\n353:  * <li>Use the offset provided with each {@link ConsumerRecord} to save your position.\n354:  * <li>On restart restore the position of the consumer using {@link #seek(TopicPartition, long)}.\n355:  * </ul>\n356:  *\n357:  * <p>\n358:  * This type of usage is simplest when the partition assignment is also done manually (this would be likely in the\n359:  * search index use case described above). If the partition assignment is done automatically special care is\n360:  * needed to handle the case where partition assignments change. This can be done by providing a\n361:  * {@link ConsumerRebalanceListener} instance in the call to {@link #subscribe(Collection, ConsumerRebalanceListener)}\n362:  * and {@link #subscribe(Pattern, ConsumerRebalanceListener)}.\n363:  * For example, when partitions are taken from a consumer the consumer will want to commit its offset for those partitions by\n364:  * implementing {@link ConsumerRebalanceListener#onPartitionsRevoked(Collection)}. When partitions are assigned to a\n365:  * consumer, the consumer will want to look up the offset for those new partitions and correctly initialize the consumer\n366:  * to that position by implementing {@link ConsumerRebalanceListener#onPartitionsAssigned(Collection)}.\n367:  * <p>\n368:  * Another common use for {@link ConsumerRebalanceListener} is to flush any caches the application maintains for\n369:  * partitions that are moved elsewhere.\n370:  *\n371:  * <h4>Controlling The Consumer's Position</h4>\n372:  *\n373:  * In most use cases the consumer will simply consume records from beginning to end, periodically committing its\n374:  * position (either automatically or manually). However Kafka allows the consumer to manually control its position,\n375:  * moving forward or backwards in a partition at will. This means a consumer can re-consume older records, or skip to\n376:  * the most recent records without actually consuming the intermediate records.\n377:  * <p>\n378:  * There are several instances where manually controlling the consumer's position can be useful.\n379:  * <p>\n380:  * One case is for time-sensitive record processing it may make sense for a consumer that falls far enough behind to not\n381:  * attempt to catch up processing all records, but rather just skip to the most recent records.\n382:  * <p>\n383:  * Another use case is for a system that maintains local state as described in the previous section. In such a system\n384:  * the consumer will want to initialize its position on start-up to whatever is contained in the local store. Likewise\n385:  * if the local state is destroyed (say because the disk is lost) the state may be recreated on a new machine by\n386:  * re-consuming all the data and recreating the state (assuming that Kafka is retaining sufficient history).\n387:  * <p>\n388:  * Kafka allows specifying the position using {@link #seek(TopicPartition, long)} to specify the new position. Special\n389:  * methods for seeking to the earliest and latest offset the server maintains are also available (\n390:  * {@link #seekToBeginning(Collection)} and {@link #seekToEnd(Collection)} respectively).\n391:  *\n392:  * <h4>Consumption Flow Control</h4>\n393:  *\n394:  * If a consumer is assigned multiple partitions to fetch data from, it will try to consume from all of them at the same time,\n395:  * effectively giving these partitions the same priority for consumption. However in some cases consumers may want to\n396:  * first focus on fetching from some subset of the assigned partitions at full speed, and only start fetching other partitions\n397:  * when these partitions have few or no data to consume.\n398:  *\n399:  * <p>\n400:  * One of such cases is stream processing, where processor fetches from two topics and performs the join on these two streams.\n401:  * When one of the topics is long lagging behind the other, the processor would like to pause fetching from the ahead topic\n402:  * in order to get the lagging stream to catch up. Another example is bootstrapping upon consumer starting up where there are\n403:  * a lot of history data to catch up, the applications usually want to get the latest data on some of the topics before consider\n404:  * fetching other topics.\n405:  *\n406:  * <p>\n407:  * Kafka supports dynamic controlling of consumption flows by using {@link #pause(Collection)} and {@link #resume(Collection)}\n408:  * to pause the consumption on the specified assigned partitions and resume the consumption\n409:  * on the specified paused partitions respectively in the future {@link #poll(Duration)} calls.\n410:  *\n411:  * <h3>Reading Transactional Messages</h3>\n412:  *\n413:  * <p>\n414:  * Transactions were introduced in Kafka 0.11.0 wherein applications can write to multiple topics and partitions atomically.\n415:  * In order for this to work, consumers reading from these partitions should be configured to only read committed data.\n416:  * This can be achieved by setting the {@code isolation.level=read_committed} in the consumer's configuration.\n417:  *\n418:  * <p>\n419:  * In <code>read_committed</code> mode, the consumer will read only those transactional messages which have been\n420:  * successfully committed. It will continue to read non-transactional messages as before. There is no client-side\n421:  * buffering in <code>read_committed</code> mode. Instead, the end offset of a partition for a <code>read_committed</code>\n422:  * consumer would be the offset of the first message in the partition belonging to an open transaction. This offset\n423:  * is known as the 'Last Stable Offset'(LSO).</p>\n424:  *\n425:  * <p>\n426:  * A {@code read_committed} consumer will only read up to the LSO and filter out any transactional\n427:  * messages which have been aborted. The LSO also affects the behavior of {@link #seekToEnd(Collection)} and\n428:  * {@link #endOffsets(Collection)} for {@code read_committed} consumers, details of which are in each method's documentation.\n429:  * Finally, the fetch lag metrics are also adjusted to be relative to the LSO for {@code read_committed} consumers.\n430:  *\n431:  * <p>\n432:  * Partitions with transactional messages will include commit or abort markers which indicate the result of a transaction.\n433:  * There markers are not returned to applications, yet have an offset in the log. As a result, applications reading from\n434:  * topics with transactional messages will see gaps in the consumed offsets. These missing messages would be the transaction\n435:  * markers, and they are filtered out for consumers in both isolation levels. Additionally, applications using\n436:  * {@code read_committed} consumers may also see gaps due to aborted transactions, since those messages would not\n437:  * be returned by the consumer and yet would have valid offsets.\n438:  *\n439:  * <h3><a name=\"multithreaded\">Multi-threaded Processing</a></h3>\n440:  *\n441:  * The Kafka consumer is NOT thread-safe. It is the responsibility of the user to ensure that multi-threaded access\n442:  * is properly synchronized. Un-synchronized access will result in {@link ConcurrentModificationException}.\n443:  *\n444:  * <p>\n445:  * The only exception to this rule is {@link #wakeup()}, which can safely be used from an external thread to\n446:  * interrupt an active operation. In this case, a {@link org.apache.kafka.common.errors.WakeupException} will be\n447:  * thrown from the thread blocking on the operation. This can be used to shutdown the consumer from another thread.\n448:  * The following snippet shows the typical pattern:\n449:  *\n450:  * <pre>\n451:  * {@code\n452:  * public class KafkaConsumerRunner implements Runnable {\n453:  *     private final AtomicBoolean closed = new AtomicBoolean(false);\n454:  *     private final KafkaConsumer consumer;\n455:  *\n456:  *     public KafkaConsumerRunner(KafkaConsumer consumer) {\n457:  *       this.consumer = consumer;\n458:  *     }\n459:  *\n460:  *     @Override\n461:  *     public void run() {\n462:  *         try {\n463:  *             consumer.subscribe(Arrays.asList(\"topic\"));\n464:  *             while (!closed.get()) {\n465:  *                 ConsumerRecords records = consumer.poll(Duration.ofMillis(10000));\n466:  *                 // Handle new records\n467:  *             }\n468:  *         } catch (WakeupException e) {\n469:  *             // Ignore exception if closing\n470:  *             if (!closed.get()) throw e;\n471:  *         } finally {\n472:  *             consumer.close();\n473:  *         }\n474:  *     }\n475:  *\n476:  *     // Shutdown hook which can be called from a separate thread\n477:  *     public void shutdown() {\n478:  *         closed.set(true);\n479:  *         consumer.wakeup();\n480:  *     }\n481:  * }\n482:  * }</pre>\n483:  *\n484:  * Then in a separate thread, the consumer can be shutdown by setting the closed flag and waking up the consumer.\n485:  *\n486:  * <p>\n487:  * <pre>\n488:  * {@code\n489:  *     closed.set(true);\n490:  *     consumer.wakeup();\n491:  * }</pre>\n492:  *\n493:  * <p>\n494:  * Note that while it is possible to use thread interrupts instead of {@link #wakeup()} to abort a blocking operation\n495:  * (in which case, {@link InterruptException} will be raised), we discourage their use since they may cause a clean\n496:  * shutdown of the consumer to be aborted. Interrupts are mainly supported for those cases where using {@link #wakeup()}\n497:  * is impossible, e.g. when a consumer thread is managed by code that is unaware of the Kafka client.\n498:  *\n499:  * <p>\n500:  * We have intentionally avoided implementing a particular threading model for processing. This leaves several\n501:  * options for implementing multi-threaded processing of records.\n502:  *\n503:  * <h4>1. One Consumer Per Thread</h4>\n504:  *\n505:  * A simple option is to give each thread its own consumer instance. Here are the pros and cons of this approach:\n506:  * <ul>\n507:  * <li><b>PRO</b>: It is the easiest to implement\n508:  * <li><b>PRO</b>: It is often the fastest as no inter-thread co-ordination is needed\n509:  * <li><b>PRO</b>: It makes in-order processing on a per-partition basis very easy to implement (each thread just\n510:  * processes messages in the order it receives them).\n511:  * <li><b>CON</b>: More consumers means more TCP connections to the cluster (one per thread). In general Kafka handles\n512:  * connections very efficiently so this is generally a small cost.\n513:  * <li><b>CON</b>: Multiple consumers means more requests being sent to the server and slightly less batching of data\n514:  * which can cause some drop in I/O throughput.\n515:  * <li><b>CON</b>: The number of total threads across all processes will be limited by the total number of partitions.\n516:  * </ul>\n517:  *\n518:  * <h4>2. Decouple Consumption and Processing</h4>\n519:  *\n520:  * Another alternative is to have one or more consumer threads that do all data consumption and hands off\n521:  * {@link ConsumerRecords} instances to a blocking queue consumed by a pool of processor threads that actually handle\n522:  * the record processing.\n523:  *\n524:  * This option likewise has pros and cons:\n525:  * <ul>\n526:  * <li><b>PRO</b>: This option allows independently scaling the number of consumers and processors. This makes it\n527:  * possible to have a single consumer that feeds many processor threads, avoiding any limitation on partitions.\n528:  * <li><b>CON</b>: Guaranteeing order across the processors requires particular care as the threads will execute\n529:  * independently an earlier chunk of data may actually be processed after a later chunk of data just due to the luck of\n530:  * thread execution timing. For processing that has no ordering requirements this is not a problem.\n531:  * <li><b>CON</b>: Manually committing the position becomes harder as it requires that all threads co-ordinate to ensure\n532:  * that processing is complete for that partition.\n533:  * </ul>\n534:  *\n535:  * There are many possible variations on this approach. For example each processor thread can have its own queue, and\n536:  * the consumer threads can hash into these queues using the TopicPartition to ensure in-order consumption and simplify\n537:  * commit.\n538:  */\n539: public class KafkaConsumer<K, V> implements Consumer<K, V> {\n540: \n541:     private static final ConsumerDelegateCreator CREATOR = new ConsumerDelegateCreator();\n542: \n543:     private final ConsumerDelegate<K, V> delegate;\n544: \n545:     /**\n546:      * A consumer is instantiated by providing a set of key-value pairs as configuration. Valid configuration strings\n547:      * are documented <a href=\"http://kafka.apache.org/documentation.html#consumerconfigs\" >here</a>. Values can be\n548:      * either strings or objects of the appropriate type (for example a numeric configuration would accept either the\n549:      * string \"42\" or the integer 42).\n550:      * <p>\n551:      * Valid configuration strings are documented at {@link ConsumerConfig}.\n552:      * <p>\n553:      * Note: after creating a {@code KafkaConsumer} you must always {@link #close()} it to avoid resource leaks.\n554:      *\n555:      * @param configs The consumer configs\n556:      */\n557:     public KafkaConsumer(Map<String, Object> configs) {\n558:         this(configs, null, null);\n559:     }\n560: \n561:     /**\n562:      * A consumer is instantiated by providing a {@link java.util.Properties} object as configuration.\n563:      * <p>\n564:      * Valid configuration strings are documented at {@link ConsumerConfig}.\n565:      * <p>\n566:      * Note: after creating a {@code KafkaConsumer} you must always {@link #close()} it to avoid resource leaks.\n567:      *\n568:      * @param properties The consumer configuration properties\n569:      */\n570:     public KafkaConsumer(Properties properties) {\n571:         this(properties, null, null);\n572:     }\n573: \n574:     /**\n575:      * A consumer is instantiated by providing a {@link java.util.Properties} object as configuration, and a\n576:      * key and a value {@link Deserializer}.\n577:      * <p>\n578:      * Valid configuration strings are documented at {@link ConsumerConfig}.\n579:      * <p>\n580:      * Note: after creating a {@code KafkaConsumer} you must always {@link #close()} it to avoid resource leaks.\n581:      *\n582:      * @param properties The consumer configuration properties\n583:      * @param keyDeserializer The deserializer for key that implements {@link Deserializer}. The configure() method\n584:      *            won't be called in the consumer when the deserializer is passed in directly.\n585:      * @param valueDeserializer The deserializer for value that implements {@link Deserializer}. The configure() method\n586:      *            won't be called in the consumer when the deserializer is passed in directly.\n587:      */\n588:     public KafkaConsumer(Properties properties,\n589:                          Deserializer<K> keyDeserializer,\n590:                          Deserializer<V> valueDeserializer) {\n591:         this(propsToMap(properties), keyDeserializer, valueDeserializer);\n592:     }\n593: \n594:     /**\n595:      * A consumer is instantiated by providing a set of key-value pairs as configuration, and a key and a value {@link Deserializer}.\n596:      * <p>\n597:      * Valid configuration strings are documented at {@link ConsumerConfig}.\n598:      * <p>\n599:      * Note: after creating a {@code KafkaConsumer} you must always {@link #close()} it to avoid resource leaks.\n600:      *\n601:      * @param configs The consumer configs\n602:      * @param keyDeserializer The deserializer for key that implements {@link Deserializer}. The configure() method\n603:      *            won't be called in the consumer when the deserializer is passed in directly.\n604:      * @param valueDeserializer The deserializer for value that implements {@link Deserializer}. The configure() method\n605:      *            won't be called in the consumer when the deserializer is passed in directly.\n606:      */\n607:     public KafkaConsumer(Map<String, Object> configs,\n608:                          Deserializer<K> keyDeserializer,\n609:                          Deserializer<V> valueDeserializer) {\n610:         this(new ConsumerConfig(ConsumerConfig.appendDeserializerToConfig(configs, keyDeserializer, valueDeserializer)),\n611:                 keyDeserializer, valueDeserializer);\n612:     }\n613: \n614:     KafkaConsumer(ConsumerConfig config, Deserializer<K> keyDeserializer, Deserializer<V> valueDeserializer) {\n615:         delegate = CREATOR.create(config, keyDeserializer, valueDeserializer);\n616:     }\n617: \n618:     KafkaConsumer(LogContext logContext,\n619:                   Time time,\n620:                   ConsumerConfig config,\n621:                   Deserializer<K> keyDeserializer,\n622:                   Deserializer<V> valueDeserializer,\n623:                   KafkaClient client,\n624:                   SubscriptionState subscriptions,\n625:                   ConsumerMetadata metadata,\n626:                   List<ConsumerPartitionAssignor> assignors) {\n627:         delegate = CREATOR.create(\n628:             logContext,\n629:             time,\n630:             config,\n631:             keyDeserializer,\n632:             valueDeserializer,\n633:             client,\n634:             subscriptions,\n635:             metadata,\n636:             assignors\n637:         );\n638:     }\n639: \n640:     /**\n641:      * Get the set of partitions currently assigned to this consumer. If subscription happened by directly assigning\n642:      * partitions using {@link #assign(Collection)} then this will simply return the same partitions that\n643:      * were assigned. If topic subscription was used, then this will give the set of topic partitions currently assigned\n644:      * to the consumer (which may be none if the assignment hasn't happened yet, or the partitions are in the\n645:      * process of getting reassigned).\n646:      * @return The set of partitions currently assigned to this consumer\n647:      */\n648:     public Set<TopicPartition> assignment() {\n649:         return delegate.assignment();\n650:     }\n651: \n652:     /**\n653:      * Get the current subscription. Will return the same topics used in the most recent call to\n654:      * {@link #subscribe(Collection, ConsumerRebalanceListener)}, or an empty set if no such call has been made.\n655:      * @return The set of topics currently subscribed to\n656:      */\n657:     public Set<String> subscription() {\n658:         return delegate.subscription();\n659:     }\n660: \n661:     /**\n662:      * Subscribe to the given list of topics to get dynamically\n663:      * assigned partitions. <b>Topic subscriptions are not incremental. This list will replace the current\n664:      * assignment (if there is one).</b> Note that it is not possible to combine topic subscription with group management\n665:      * with manual partition assignment through {@link #assign(Collection)}.\n666:      *\n667:      * If the given list of topics is empty, it is treated the same as {@link #unsubscribe()}.\n668:      *\n669:      * <p>\n670:      * As part of group management, the group coordinator will keep track of the list of consumers that belong to a particular\n671:      * group and will trigger a rebalance operation if any one of the following events are triggered:\n672:      * <ul>\n673:      * <li>Number of partitions change for any of the subscribed topics\n674:      * <li>A subscribed topic is created or deleted\n675:      * <li>An existing member of the consumer group is shutdown or fails\n676:      * <li>A new member is added to the consumer group\n677:      * </ul>\n678:      * <p>\n679:      * When any of these events are triggered, the provided listener will be invoked in this way:\n680:      * <ul>\n681:      *     <li>{@link ConsumerRebalanceListener#onPartitionsRevoked(Collection)} will be invoked with the partitions to revoke, before re-assigning those partitions to another consumer.</li>\n682:      *     <li>{@link ConsumerRebalanceListener#onPartitionsAssigned(Collection)} will be invoked when the rebalance completes (even if no new partitions are assigned to the consumer)</li>\n683:      * </ul>\n684:      * Note that rebalances will only occur during an active call to {@link #poll(Duration)}, so callbacks will\n685:      * also only be invoked during that time.\n686:      *\n687:      * The provided listener will immediately override any listener set in a previous call to subscribe.\n688:      * It is guaranteed, however, that the partitions revoked/assigned through this interface are from topics\n689:      * subscribed in this call. See {@link ConsumerRebalanceListener} for more details.\n690:      *\n691:      * @param topics The list of topics to subscribe to\n692:      * @param listener Non-null listener instance to get notifications on partition assignment/revocation for the\n693:      *                 subscribed topics\n694:      * @throws IllegalArgumentException If topics is null or contains null or empty elements, or if listener is null\n695:      * @throws IllegalStateException If {@code subscribe()} is called previously with pattern, or assign is called\n696:      *                               previously (without a subsequent call to {@link #unsubscribe()}), or if not\n697:      *                               configured at-least one partition assignment strategy\n698:      */\n699:     @Override\n700:     public void subscribe(Collection<String> topics, ConsumerRebalanceListener listener) {\n701:         delegate.subscribe(topics, listener);\n702:     }\n703: \n704:     /**\n705:      * Subscribe to the given list of topics to get dynamically assigned partitions.\n706:      * <b>Topic subscriptions are not incremental. This list will replace the current\n707:      * assignment (if there is one).</b> It is not possible to combine topic subscription with group management\n708:      * with manual partition assignment through {@link #assign(Collection)}.\n709:      *\n710:      * If the given list of topics is empty, it is treated the same as {@link #unsubscribe()}.\n711:      *\n712:      * <p>\n713:      * This is a short-hand for {@link #subscribe(Collection, ConsumerRebalanceListener)}, which\n714:      * uses a no-op listener. If you need the ability to seek to particular offsets, you should prefer\n715:      * {@link #subscribe(Collection, ConsumerRebalanceListener)}, since group rebalances will cause partition offsets\n716:      * to be reset. You should also provide your own listener if you are doing your own offset\n717:      * management since the listener gives you an opportunity to commit offsets before a rebalance finishes.\n718:      *\n719:      * @param topics The list of topics to subscribe to\n720:      * @throws IllegalArgumentException If topics is null or contains null or empty elements\n721:      * @throws IllegalStateException If {@code subscribe()} is called previously with pattern, or assign is called\n722:      *                               previously (without a subsequent call to {@link #unsubscribe()}), or if not\n723:      *                               configured at-least one partition assignment strategy\n724:      */\n725:     @Override\n726:     public void subscribe(Collection<String> topics) {\n727:         delegate.subscribe(topics);\n728:     }\n729: \n730:     /**\n731:      * Subscribe to all topics matching specified pattern to get dynamically assigned partitions.\n732:      * The pattern matching will be done periodically against all topics existing at the time of check.\n733:      * This can be controlled through the {@code metadata.max.age.ms} configuration: by lowering\n734:      * the max metadata age, the consumer will refresh metadata more often and check for matching topics.\n735:      * <p>\n736:      * See {@link #subscribe(Collection, ConsumerRebalanceListener)} for details on the\n737:      * use of the {@link ConsumerRebalanceListener}. Generally rebalances are triggered when there\n738:      * is a change to the topics matching the provided pattern and when consumer group membership changes.\n739:      * Group rebalances only take place during an active call to {@link #poll(Duration)}.\n740:      *\n741:      * @param pattern Pattern to subscribe to\n742:      * @param listener Non-null listener instance to get notifications on partition assignment/revocation for the\n743:      *                 subscribed topics\n744:      * @throws IllegalArgumentException If pattern or listener is null\n745:      * @throws IllegalStateException If {@code subscribe()} is called previously with topics, or assign is called\n746:      *                               previously (without a subsequent call to {@link #unsubscribe()}), or if not\n747:      *                               configured at-least one partition assignment strategy\n748:      */\n749:     @Override\n750:     public void subscribe(Pattern pattern, ConsumerRebalanceListener listener) {\n751:         delegate.subscribe(pattern, listener);\n752:     }\n753: \n754:     /**\n755:      * Subscribe to all topics matching specified pattern to get dynamically assigned partitions.\n756:      * The pattern matching will be done periodically against topics existing at the time of check.\n757:      * <p>\n758:      * This is a short-hand for {@link #subscribe(Pattern, ConsumerRebalanceListener)}, which\n759:      * uses a no-op listener. If you need the ability to seek to particular offsets, you should prefer\n760:      * {@link #subscribe(Pattern, ConsumerRebalanceListener)}, since group rebalances will cause partition offsets\n761:      * to be reset. You should also provide your own listener if you are doing your own offset\n762:      * management since the listener gives you an opportunity to commit offsets before a rebalance finishes.\n763:      *\n764:      * @param pattern Pattern to subscribe to\n765:      * @throws IllegalArgumentException If pattern is null\n766:      * @throws IllegalStateException If {@code subscribe()} is called previously with topics, or assign is called\n767:      *                               previously (without a subsequent call to {@link #unsubscribe()}), or if not\n768:      *                               configured at-least one partition assignment strategy\n769:      */\n770:     @Override\n771:     public void subscribe(Pattern pattern) {\n772:         delegate.subscribe(pattern);\n773:     }\n774: \n775:     /**\n776:      * Subscribe to all topics matching the specified pattern, to get dynamically assigned partitions.\n777:      * The pattern matching will be done periodically against all topics. This is only supported under the\n778:      * CONSUMER group protocol (see {@link ConsumerConfig#GROUP_PROTOCOL_CONFIG}).\n779:      * <p>\n780:      * If the provided pattern is not compatible with Google RE2/J, an {@link InvalidRegularExpression} will be\n781:      * eventually thrown on a call to {@link #poll(Duration)} following this call to subscribe.\n782:      * <p>\n783:      * See {@link #subscribe(Collection, ConsumerRebalanceListener)} for details on the\n784:      * use of the {@link ConsumerRebalanceListener}. Generally, rebalances are triggered when there\n785:      * is a change to the topics matching the provided pattern and when consumer group membership changes.\n786:      * Group rebalances only take place during an active call to {@link #poll(Duration)}.\n787:      *\n788:      * @param pattern  Pattern to subscribe to, that must be compatible with Google RE2/J.\n789:      * @param listener Non-null listener instance to get notifications on partition assignment/revocation for the\n790:      *                 subscribed topics.\n791:      * @throws IllegalArgumentException If pattern is null or empty, or if the listener is null.\n792:      * @throws IllegalStateException    If {@code subscribe()} is called previously with topics, or assign is called\n793:      *                                  previously (without a subsequent call to {@link #unsubscribe()}).\n794:      */\n795:     @Override\n796:     public void subscribe(SubscriptionPattern pattern, ConsumerRebalanceListener listener) {\n797:         delegate.subscribe(pattern, listener);\n798:     }\n799: \n800:     /**\n801:      * Subscribe to all topics matching the specified pattern, to get dynamically assigned partitions.\n802:      * The pattern matching will be done periodically against topics. This is only supported under the\n803:      * CONSUMER group protocol (see {@link ConsumerConfig#GROUP_PROTOCOL_CONFIG})\n804:      * <p>\n805:      * If the provided pattern is not compatible with Google RE2/J, an {@link InvalidRegularExpression} will be\n806:      * eventually thrown on a call to {@link #poll(Duration)} following this call to subscribe.\n807:      * <p>\n808:      * This is a short-hand for {@link #subscribe(Pattern, ConsumerRebalanceListener)}, which\n809:      * uses a no-op listener. If you need the ability to seek to particular offsets, you should prefer\n810:      * {@link #subscribe(Pattern, ConsumerRebalanceListener)}, since group rebalances will cause partition offsets\n811:      * to be reset. You should also provide your own listener if you are doing your own offset\n812:      * management since the listener gives you an opportunity to commit offsets before a rebalance finishes.\n813:      *\n814:      * @param pattern Pattern to subscribe to, that must be compatible with Google RE2/J.\n815:      * @throws IllegalArgumentException If pattern is null or empty.\n816:      * @throws IllegalStateException    If {@code subscribe()} is called previously with topics, or assign is called\n817:      *                                  previously (without a subsequent call to {@link #unsubscribe()}).\n818:      */\n819:     @Override\n820:     public void subscribe(SubscriptionPattern pattern) {\n821:         delegate.subscribe(pattern);\n822:     }\n823: \n824:     /**\n825:      * Unsubscribe from topics currently subscribed with {@link #subscribe(Collection)} or {@link #subscribe(Pattern)}.\n826:      * This also clears any partitions directly assigned through {@link #assign(Collection)}.\n827:      *\n828:      * @throws org.apache.kafka.common.KafkaException for any other unrecoverable errors (e.g. rebalance callback errors)\n829:      */\n830:     public void unsubscribe() {\n831:         delegate.unsubscribe();\n832:     }\n833: \n834:     /**\n835:      * Manually assign a list of partitions to this consumer. This interface does not allow for incremental assignment\n836:      * and will replace the previous assignment (if there is one).\n837:      * <p>\n838:      * If the given list of topic partitions is empty, it is treated the same as {@link #unsubscribe()}.\n839:      * <p>\n840:      * Manual topic assignment through this method does not use the consumer's group management\n841:      * functionality. As such, there will be no rebalance operation triggered when group membership or cluster and topic\n842:      * metadata change. Note that it is not possible to use both manual partition assignment with {@link #assign(Collection)}\n843:      * and group assignment with {@link #subscribe(Collection, ConsumerRebalanceListener)}.\n844:      * <p>\n845:      * If auto-commit is enabled, an async commit (based on the old assignment) will be triggered before the new\n846:      * assignment replaces the old one.\n847:      *\n848:      * @param partitions The list of partitions to assign this consumer\n849:      * @throws IllegalArgumentException If partitions is null or contains null or empty topics\n850:      * @throws IllegalStateException If {@code subscribe()} is called previously with topics or pattern\n851:      *                               (without a subsequent call to {@link #unsubscribe()})\n852:      */\n853:     @Override\n854:     public void assign(Collection<TopicPartition> partitions) {\n855:         delegate.assign(partitions);\n856:     }\n857: \n858:     /**\n859:      * Fetch data for the topics or partitions specified using one of the subscribe/assign APIs. It is an error to not have\n860:      * subscribed to any topics or partitions before polling for data.\n861:      * <p>\n862:      * On each poll, consumer will try to use the last consumed offset as the starting offset and fetch sequentially. The last\n863:      * consumed offset can be manually set through {@link #seek(TopicPartition, long)} or automatically set as the last committed\n864:      * offset for the subscribed list of partitions\n865:      *\n866:      * <p>\n867:      * This method returns immediately if there are records available or if the position advances past control records\n868:      * or aborted transactions when isolation.level=read_committed.\n869:      * Otherwise, it will await the passed timeout. If the timeout expires, an empty record set will be returned.\n870:      * Note that this method may block beyond the timeout in order to execute custom\n871:      * {@link ConsumerRebalanceListener} callbacks.\n872:      *\n873:      *\n874:      * @param timeout The maximum time to block (must not be greater than {@link Long#MAX_VALUE} milliseconds)\n875:      *\n876:      * @return map of topic to records since the last fetch for the subscribed list of topics and partitions\n877:      *\n878:      * @throws org.apache.kafka.clients.consumer.InvalidOffsetException if the offset for a partition or set of\n879:      *             partitions is undefined or out of range and no offset reset policy has been configured\n880:      * @throws org.apache.kafka.common.errors.WakeupException if {@link #wakeup()} is called before or while this\n881:      *             function is called\n882:      * @throws org.apache.kafka.common.errors.InterruptException if the calling thread is interrupted before or while\n883:      *             this function is called\n884:      * @throws org.apache.kafka.common.errors.AuthenticationException if authentication fails. See the exception for more details\n885:      * @throws org.apache.kafka.common.errors.AuthorizationException if caller lacks Read access to any of the subscribed\n886:      *             topics or to the configured groupId. This offset acts as a unique identifier of\\n73:  * a record within that partition, and also denotes the position of the consumer in the partition. For example, a consumer\\n74:  * which is at position 5 has consumed records with offsets 0 through 4 and will next receive the record with offset 5.\\n75:  * Note that offsets are not guaranteed to be consecutive (such as compacted topic or when records have been produced\\n76:  * using transactions). These processes can either be running on the same machine or they can be\\n97:  * distributed over many machines to provide scalability and fault tolerance for processing. This is achieved by balancing the partitions between all\\n103:  * members in the consumer group so that each partition is assigned to exactly one consumer in the group. So if there\\n104:  * is a topic with four partitions, and a consumer group with two processes, each process would consume from two partitions.\\n105:  * <p>\\n106:  * Membership in a consumer group is maintained dynamically: if a process fails, the partitions assigned to it will\\n107:  * be reassigned to other consumers in the same group. To get semantics similar to\\n119:  * a queue in a traditional messaging system all processes would be part of a single consumer group and hence record\\n120:  * delivery would be balanced over the group like with a queue. Unlike a traditional messaging system, though, you can\\n121:  * have multiple such groups.
